{{c1::SOX-9}} is a key transcription factor involved in the differentiation of cells towards the {{c2::cartilage}} lineage	SOX-9 binds to a critical consensus sequence in the collagen 2 (Col2) promoter to activate its transcription.  Formation of the cartilage template involves a multi-step process in which prechondrogenic mesenchymal cells form condensations prior to differentiating into matrix-producing chondroblasts.   Retinoids, particularly retinoic acid, are among the numerous signaling molecules that have been implicated in this process.  Efforts aimed at understanding the mechanisms by which expression of retinoic acid receptor attenuates chondroblast differentiation led to the discovery of the transcriptional activity of SOX-9.										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::BasicScience::MSK_Science::BiologicTissues::Cartilage"

What is the Usual Mechanism of Dislocation in a Spastic CP Hip?  In CP, the {{c1::Adductors and Iliopsoas}} are Spastic and Shortened, and {{c1::Glut Max and Medius}} are Weak The Center of Movement is translated from the Center of the {{c1::Femoral Head}} distally to the level of the {{c1::Lesser Troch}} The Hip Joint Capsule is Elongated {{c1::Posterosuperiorly}}, with Gradual Dislocation of the hip											"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Peds::Syndromes::CP::Hip"
How is Joint Reaction Force Defined?  {{c1::Force Generated within a Joint in Response to Forces Acting on the Joint}}	In the hip, it is the result of the need to balance the moment arms of the body weight and abductor tension - Maintains a Level Pelvis								Orthobullets: Hip Biomechanics		"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Recon::Hip::Anatomy_Biomechanics::Biomechanics"
The Instant Center of Rotation is the {{c1::Point about which a Joint Rotates}}   Often Changes During {{c1::Rotation}} due to {{c1::Joint Translation}}	Center of Gravity of Human is just ANTERIOR to S2								Orthobullets: Hip Biomechanics		"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Recon::Hip::Anatomy_Biomechanics::Biomechanics"

What is the most Sensitive and Specific test for Subscapularis pathology?  {{c1::Internal Rotation Lag Sign}}	Stand behind patient, Flex Elbow to 90°, hold Shoulder at 20° Elevation and 20° Extension. Internally Rotate Shoulder to near Maximum holding the Wrist by Passively lifting the Dorsum of the Hand away from the Lumbar Spine – then supporting the Elbow, tell patient to Maintain position and Release the Wrist while looking for a Lag. INABILITY to do this indicates Subscapularis pathology. Is confounded by other muscles. }}	More Accurate if the Tested Hand can reach the Contralateral Scapula.  More Accurate for INFERIOR portion of Subscapularis								Orthobullets: Shoulder Exam		"#Ortho::Orthobullets::ShoulderElbow::Shoulder::Anatomy_Eval::Exam"

"How do you perform an Active Compression test (""O'Brien's Test"") for Labral and SLAP injuries?  {{c1::Patient Forward Flexes the affected arm to 90 degrees while keeping the Elbow Fully Extended. The Arm is then ADducted 10-15 degrees ACROSS the body. The patient then PRONATES the Forearm so the Thumb is pointing DOWN. The examiner applies Downward force to the Wrist while the arm is in this position while the Patient Resists. The patient then Supinates the Forearm so the Palm is Up and the examiner once again applies Force to the Wrist while the Patient Resists.}}"	"Positive for SLAP tear when there is Pain ""Deep"" in the Glenohumeral Joint while the Forearm is PRONATED but NOT when the Forearm is Supinated"								Orthobullets: Shoulder Exam		"#Ortho::Orthobullets::ShoulderElbow::Shoulder::Anatomy_Eval::Exam"

With Treatment of Subtrochanteric Femur Fractures, How can a Blade Plate function as a Tension Band Construct?  {{c1::Femur Eccentrically Loaded with Tensile force on the Lateral cortex converted to Compressive force on Medial cortex}}	In order for a fracture to be successfully treated with tension band principles the bone must be eccentrically loaded, the construct must be applied on the tensile side, and the opposite cortex must be able to withstand compressive forces.   Tension bands function by converting distraction forces into compressive forces that are evenly distributed across a fracture site, to create an environment favourable for fracture healing. What is the General Treatment for Paprosky Type I Acetabular Defects?  {{c1::Standard Revision Components or with Cemented or Noncemented Hemispherical Cups}}	Type I Paprosky defects are characterized by Minimal Bone Loss, with the Superior and Medial Walls of the Acetabulum remaining Intact. The Hip Center of Rotation is within 5 mm of the Superior Obturator Line, and there is NO Teardrop or Ischial Osteolysis. What is the General Treatment for Paprosky Type IIA Acetabular Defects?  {{c1::Cemented or Noncemented Hemispherical Cup with Screws for Initial Stability or with a Trabecular Metal Cup}}	Type IIA Paprosky defects are characterized by MODERATE Bone Loss, with the Superior and Medial Walls of the Acetabulum remaining Intact. The Hip Center of Rotation is within 5 mm of the Superior Obturator Line, and there is NO Teardrop or Ischial Osteolysis.   However, there is a Loss of Bone in the Anterior and Posterior Columns, which can Compromise Component Stability. Bucholz Classification of Pediatric Acetabulum Fractures  {{c1::Shearing - SH I or II}} {{c1::Crushing/Impaction - SH V}}		The Bucholz classification divides injury patterns into two groups.   The first group involves a shearing injury causing a Salter-Harris Type 1 or Type 2 injury to the triradiate cartilage.  The second group is a crush injury or Salter-Harris Type 5 injury to the triradiate cartilage. The Bucholz classification has been modified to also include a Type 4 injury involving the metaphysis on either side of the triradiate cartilage and a  Type 6 injury where a hematoma forms adjacent to the triradiate cartilage resulting in bony bridge formation.							Orthobullets: Pediatric Pelvis Fractures		"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Peds::Trauma::Pelvis_Femur::Pelvis"
